<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/1369-9261/1998">New J. Chem.</a></span>, 2024, <strong>48</strong>, 4088-4095</div><h1 id="sect151"><span class="title_heading">Enhancing the performance of NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries through graphite incorporation and polyethylene glycol 6000 modification</span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Mengtao 
            Li
          
        
      </span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Jinghe 
            Cao
          
        
      </span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup><span class="bold"> and </span>
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Junke 
            Ou
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Junke&nbsp;Ou (orcid.org/0000-0003-3946-266X) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3946-266X"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*<sup><span class="sup_ref italic">abc</span></sup>
      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">School of Mechanical Engineering, Chengdu University, Shiling Town, Chengdu 610106, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:ojk0001@163.com">ojk0001@163.com</a></span>
      <br><a id="affb"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Key Laboratory of Advanced Energy Materials Chemistry (Ministry of Education), College of Chemistry, Nankai University, Tianjin 300071, China</span>
      <br><a id="affc"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">c</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Institute for Advanced Study, Chengdu University, Shiling Town, Chengdu 610106, China</span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      18th December 2023
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 19th January 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 12th February 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F has become a focal point in the realm of sodium-ion battery cathode materials owing to its robust structure, elevated voltage platform, and considerable theoretical capacity. Nonetheless, the limited conductivity of NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F poses a challenge to its practical utilization. In this study, we introduce a straightforward one-step ball milling process to address this issue by creating NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F/G (G stands for graphite) and enhancing electronic conductivity through the incorporation of polyethylene glycol 6000 (PEG6K). Graphite has the capability to enhance electrochemical properties and markedly enhance electronic conductivity. Furthermore, the incorporation of PEG6K can enhance the transport stability of sodium ions at the interface of sodium-ion batteries. After optimization, NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F/G@P (P stands for PEG6K) delivers a high capacity of 128.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2C and a high rate capacity of 105.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10C, with a capacity retention rate of 116.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 200 cycles. Moreover, when coupled with a HC anode (HC as the negative electrode), the full cell still displays an outstanding performance of 76.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 20C. A simple approach is provided in our study that has the potential to significantly improve the performance of sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect211"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gained recognition as promising alternatives for large-scale energy storage systems. Their appeal stems from the abundant availability of sodium and the associated lower costs compared to other battery technologies.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–4</span></sup></a> Nonetheless, a significant hurdle hindering the widespread adoption of SIBs in commercial applications is the scarcity of suitable cathode materials. Hence, the quest for appropriate cathode materials that exhibit high-rate performance, long-term cycling stability, safety, and environmental friendliness remains a formidable challenge.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5–7</span></sup></a> Various cathode materials for SIBs have been explored, and layered metal oxides,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8</span></sup></a> organic materials,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> Prussian blue analogues,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> and polyanion types<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> have been extensively studied. Among these materials, researchers have been particularly intrigued by the stable three-dimensional (3D) open frame structure of polyanion-based cathode materials, like Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small>, NaFePO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F, and Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>F<small><sub>3</sub></small>. This unique structural characteristic has garnered significant attention in the scientific community.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12–14</span></sup></a> Specifically, NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F (NVPF) has demonstrated significant potential as a cathode material for SIBs. This is attributed to its high capacity, safe application, and notably high operating potential of 143 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15–17</span></sup></a> Nevertheless, NVPF faces a challenge in terms of low intrinsic electron conductivity, a factor that significantly impedes its electrochemical performance, affecting cycling stability and rate capability. This limitation poses a hurdle to its widespread commercial application.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,19</span></sup></a> To address the issue of NVPF's inadequate electronic conductivity, researchers have explored various strategies. These approaches encompass surface carbon coating materials, multi-pore carbon materials, engineering nanostructures, and adding other ions. These techniques aim to enhance the material's electrochemical performance and expand its potential applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20–26</span></sup></a> As anticipated, these strategies have succeeded in augmenting conductivity, leading to observable enhancements in cycling performance and rate capability to a certain extent. Nonetheless, it's worth noting that the reported NVPF composite materials can be costly, or they may fall short of meeting the commercial application demands for highly stringent energy storage because of their original discharge specific capacity being significantly lower than their theoretical specific capacity. Additional research is necessary to enhance the sodium storage capacity of NVPF without compromising capacity at higher current densities. This improvement is crucial for the commercialization of NVPF, and a key objective is to achieve this through a simplified synthesis strategy.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">In this study, we introduce a straightforward one-step ball milling approach for the preparation of materials. This method involves the incorporation of graphite as a conductive carbon material and PEG6K as a binder into NVPF. Among these components, graphite, due to its cost-effectiveness, serves as an economical conductive carbon material, leading to an enhancement in the electrochemical performance of NVPF. The increased conductivity facilitates more rapid and efficient electron transport;<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,27</span></sup></a> PEG6K is a general term for ethylene glycol polymers containing a large number of hydroxyl groups, NVPF and graphite composites are combined with hydroxyl groups with an adhesive function in PEG6K under the incorporation of PEG6K, so that the graphite sheet layer is more firmly attached to the NVPF sample, thereby enhancing the coordination strength between Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions and other molecules within the raw material. It also inhibits volatilization, while the presence of –OH functional groups improves the wettability of sodium metal and restricts dendrite growth. Consequently, this results in the construction of a stable interfacial ion transport channel, which in turn enhances ion conductivity and the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28–31</span></sup></a> Therefore, after optimization, the NVPF/G@P cathode of sodium-ion batteries exhibits an excellent reversible capacity (128.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2C), outstanding rate performance (105.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10C) and extraordinary cycling performance (116.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 200 cycles). In addition, when hard carbon is assembled as a negative electrode into a full cell, it exhibits outstanding cycling performance and highly stable rate performance.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect245"><span class="a_heading">2. Details of experiments</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect248"><span class="b_heading">2.1 Material fabrication</span></h3>
        <span>Using NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>VO<small><sub>3</sub></small> (99.9%), NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>PO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (99.9%) and NaF (99.9%) as raw materials, the carbon-coated NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F (NVPF/G@P) wrapped with PEG6K as a binder and graphite was prepared through a straightforward one-step ball milling method. The molar ratio of the three samples was 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. The carbon sources with PEG6K (stoichiometric ratio 7.5%) and graphite (stoichiometric ratio 7.5%) as binders are first crushed homogeneously in a mortar. After that, the above raw materials are put into an agate jar with a ratio of 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">20 (weight ratio) and an appropriate amount of alcohol is added, and the ball is milled at high speed for 3 h under a ball mill at 1200 rpm (MSK-SFM-3). The precursors are then placed in a vacuum oven at 100 °C to dry for 12 hours, and the dried sample is ground into a powder. Under a constant Ar atmosphere, these samples are initially calcined at 350 °C for 4 h, and next annealed at 750 °C for 6 h before cooling to room temperature. The synthesis process is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to scheme" href="#imgsch1">Scheme 1</a>. For comparison, NVPF and graphite-coated NVPF were prepared in the same preparation process as the experimental group, which were expressed as NVPF and NVPF/G, individually. Finally, in this study, commercial hard carbon is used as the anode to match the synthetic material with the whole battery, showing the practical application of sodium-ion batteries.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgsch1"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-s1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-s1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-s1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Scheme 1 </b> <span id="sch1"><span class="graphic_title">Preparation schematic of NVPF/G@P composites.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect261"><span class="b_heading">2.2 Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The X-ray diffraction pattern, thermogravimetric analysis pattern and Raman spectrum pattern were obtained using a Tong da diffractometer, Mettler Toledo thermogravimetric analyzer and Lab RAMHR800 instrument. The morphology of the material was determined by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, Hitachi S4800) and high-power transmission electron microscopy (TEM, FEI Tenia G220, Hillsboro). A Kratos XSAM 800 instrument was used to record X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy. The CHI660e electrochemical workstation model of Shanghai Cheng Hua Company was used to record all CV tests. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted in the frequency range of 0.01 Hz to 100 kHz using the CHI650e electrochemical workstation. All assembled samples underwent 30 cycles at 1C with an open-circuit voltage, aiming to reach a quasi-equilibrium state.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect265"><span class="b_heading">2.3 Electrochemical measurement</span></h3>
        <span>The as-obtained sample was mixed with acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride at a mass ratio of 7<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 with <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) to prepare a slurry. After applying evenly to aluminum foil, it was vacuum dried at 100 °C for 12 hours, and the resulting active material supported by the material was approximately 1.5 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small> 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, dissolved in EC (vinyl carbonate)<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">DEC (divinyl carbonate) = 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 (volume ratio) as electrolyte. A sodium metal sheet plays the role of the negative electrode, and glass fiber plays the role of the separator. The Model CR2032 battery was assembled in a glove box filled with argon. At 1C = 143 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the electrode current density based on NVPF was measured. In the voltage window of 2.0–4.2 V, the electrochemical performances of the half-cells and the full-cells at different rates were explored. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was recorded in the range of 2.5–4.2 V.</span>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect273"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(a)</a>, the XRD patterns for NVPF, NVPF/G, and NVPF/G@P are shown. It is clear that all the characteristic diffraction peaks coincide with the monoclinic NVPF, this suggests that the carbon coating, which has an adhesive effect, does not alter the crystal structure of NVPF. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(b)</a>, it is shown that the carbon content of each group was determined by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA). At temperatures below 300 °C, only slight mass loss in the NVPF, NVPF/G and NVPF/G@P groups can be observed, and the large weight loss in the range of 300–600 °C is due to pyrolysis of residual carbon; it is also this factor that causes the observed weight loss in NVPF. The highest residual carbon pyrolysis content in the PEG6K group was 8.5 wt%, which was higher than that in the graphite only group (6.7 wt%), indicating that the carbon content brought by PEG6K was about 1.8 wt%. That is to say, the additional carbon source brought by PEG6K can supplement the loss caused by the graphite carbon layer in the subsequent battery charging and discharging, thereby improving the stable cycling performance of the battery. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(c)</a>, in addition to the NVPF group, the Raman spectra of the other two groups of samples show two distinct characteristic peaks, the disordered carbon (D band) at 1340 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and the graphite crystalline carbon (G band) at 1600 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. With the introduction of PEG6K in the NVPF/G@P group, a higher relative strength ratio (<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small>) was obtained (0.97 for NVPF/G and 0.95 NVPF/G@P), indicating that PEG6K could increase the degree of graphitization, which implied that the NVPF/G@P group had better graphitization crystallinity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32,33</span></sup></a></span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) XRD patterns of NVPF, NVPF/G and NVPF/G@P; (b) TGA curves of NVPF, NVPF/G and NVPF/G@P; (c) Raman spectra of NVPF, NVPF/G and NVPF/G@P.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(a)</a>, the X-ray photoelectron spectrum of NVPF/G@P is shown, with Na 1s, F 1s, O 1s, V 2p, P 2p, and C 1s peaks. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(b)</a>, © high-resolution C 1s peaks were decomposed at 284.7, 285.8, 287.5, and 288.9 eV, corresponding to C–O/C–OH, C–C, C–N, and O–C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34,35</span></sup></a> The occurrence of C–O/C–OH peaks is mainly attributed to the terminal functional groups of PEG6K. The C–O/C–OH peaks were mainly attributed to the terminal functional groups of PEG6K, and with the addition of PEG6K, the C–O/C–OH peak area was significantly higher than that of other peaks, which greatly improved the wettability of NVPF to sodium metal.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(c)</a>, the high-energy spectrum of V 2p is shown, and there are two very distinct peaks. The two characteristic peaks at 515.8 and 523.5 eV correspond to V<small><sup>3+</sup></small> of NVPF.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36,37</span></sup></a> In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(d)</a>, a distinct P 2p characteristic peak is shown at 132.9 eV and 134.2 eV related to the P–C and P–O bonds.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) XPS survey scan spectra of NVPF/G@P; XPS spectra of (b) C 1s, (c) V 2p, and (d) P 2p.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(a)–(c)</a>, SEM micrographs of NVPF, NVPF/G and NVPF/G@P are shown, and due to the addition of the covered graphitic carbon coating with PEG6K, the NVPF/G@P samples in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(c)</a> show more graphite sheet layer structure than NVPF and NVPF/G in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(a) and (b)</a>. This demonstrates that PEG6K exhibits strong bonding capabilities, effectively mitigating volume fluctuations and thereby ensuring a stable electrochemical performance. In the HRTEM image in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(d)</a>, the samples show a relatively homogeneous amorphous carbon layer of 3.5 nm on the surface of the NVPF, and the spacing of the lattice fringes is 0.25 nm, which verifies the (013) plane of NVPF.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> The EDS scanning spectra element diagram in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(e)–(j)</a> depicts the uniform distribution of C, P, O, F, Na, V, and F over NVPF/G@P. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(k)</a> shows a separately tested graphite SEM micrograph, showing the apparent lamellar structure of graphite.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">SEM images showing the surface morphology of (a) NVPF, (b) NVPF/G and (c) NVPF/G@P. (d) HRTEM data for the NVPF/G@P. (e)–(j) Corresponding elemental mapping obtained by EDS and (k) FESEM micrographs of graphite.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(a)</a>, the first discharge capacity of the positive electrode with NVPF, NVPF/G, and NVPF/G@P at 0.2C magnification is 80.2, 106.2, and 128.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(a)–(c)</a>). After 50 charge–discharge cycles, the NVPF/G@P-based electrode has a capacity of 124.6 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, which much exceeds that of the NVPF and NVPF/G electrodes (78.8 and 99.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 50 cycles, respectively). In addition, the NVPF/G@P group has a highly stable charge–discharge platform, thanks to the introduction of PEG6K. Therefore, the presence of a uniform graphite–carbon coating and the introduction of PEG6K are conducive to obtaining good electrochemical performance. The rate performance test from 0.2 to 10C and back to 0.2C can clearly show that NVPF/G@P-based batteries have excellent reversible capacity in terms of rate performance testing, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5C at 127.5, 121.5, 118.8, 115.8, and 111.3 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(d) and (e)</a>. At 10C, the high rate, the NVPF/G@P electrode still has a discharge capacity of 105.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, which is much better than the values obtained based on the NVPF and NVPF/G electrodes (27.4 and 50.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively). When the magnification is switched back to 0.2C, the NVPF/G@P electrode still has a capacity of 115.6 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(f)</a> illustrates the long-term cycling of each group of samples. Since the capacity of the NVPF/G@P battery remains at a high level after 200 cycles, in addition, in this long cycle plot, the sodium-ion battery of NVPF/G@P shows the best capacity of all the test groups in this work. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(g)</a>, the structural framework and ion transport mechanism of NVPF/G@P are briefly described, and in the NVPF/G@P group with the introduction of PEG6K and graphite, the rapid diffusion of sodium ions between the electrode and the electrolyte is seen to explain the excellent stability of NVPF/G@P charge and discharge. This is confirmed by the resistance comparison analysis in the following article. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(h)</a>, we list the data of NVPF reported in recent years and compared with our work, it can be clearly seen that whether it is the first charge and discharge efficiency or the retention of battery capacity after high magnification, it is in the lead; for<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40">ref. 40</a>, although the performance is higher than in our work, in terms of cost, our work has a significant advantage, so overall our work has greater potential.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Charge–discharge profiles of NVPF (a), NVPF/G (b) and NVPF/G@P (c) at 0.2C, C-rate discharge performance of the NVPF/G@P electrode (d), the rate performances of the three electrodes at various current densities (e), cycling performance of the NVPF samples (f), the structural framework of NVPF and the principle of ion transport (g) and performance comparison with other NVPF materials (h).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a>, the battery is assembled and tested to evaluate the electrochemical performance of the material. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(a) and (b)</a> show the NVPF/G group and the NVPF/G@P group with a scanning rate of 0.1–0.5 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, and it is evident that the redox characteristic peaks are present on both electrodes, which are due to phase transitions with sodium intercalation and NVPF extraction;<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,22</span></sup></a> in addition, the NVPF/G@P group has a higher intensity peak, indicating that after the addition of PEG6K, full combination with graphite can increase the diffusion rate of sodium ions, achieving low electrochemical polarization and extremely stable high electrochemical reversibility. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(c)</a> shows the number of triple cycles in the NVPF/G@P group with a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, and the almost identical curves indicate that the NVPF/G@P group is extremely stable. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(d)</a> shows the EIS analysis spectra for NVPF, NVPF/G, and NVPF/G@P, fitted with an equivalent circuit model (see Illustration 5d inside), and the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>p</sub></small> plotted against the square root of the scan rate (<span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small>) and the impedance spectra of three samples at 1C after 30 cycles. It is clear that the EIS spectrum is mainly composed of high-frequency intercepts (corresponding to <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>). A concave semicircle is formed between the electrolyte and the electrode at the intermediate frequency (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>, the transfer resistance <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>), while at the low frequency it appears as a straight line. We use constant phase elements (CPEs) to simulate the capacitance and Warburg impedance <span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>w</sub></small> is contributed by the surface of the active material.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> The three samples in the Nyquist plot have a half-ring at the middle and high frequencies, and a straight line at the low frequencies. The charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>) of NVPF/G@P is lower than that of the other two groups, which is conducive to fast electrochemical kinetics and excellent electrochemical performance; this phenomenon is due to the diffusion of sodium ions between the electrode and the electrolyte, and the higher the slope coefficient, the faster the diffusion of sodium ions.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42–44</span></sup></a> The fitting results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. By comparison, the internal resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> = 4.71 Ω) and charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> = 147.82 Ω) of the NVPF/G@P samples were the smallest, indicating that the addition of PEK6K combined with the graphitic carbon layer could significantly reduce the internal resistance of NVPF/G@P as well as greatly enhance charge transfer. Notably, NVPF/G@P exhibits the smallest semicircle, which shows that it has the most outstanding electrochemical properties among the materials synthesized in this paper.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> In this study, the diffusion coefficient corresponding to sodium ions was further investigated using the equation below,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> and the data calculated from this formula are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>:<br></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText"><span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>p</sub></small> = 2.69 × 10<small><sup>5</sup></small><span class="italic">n</span><small><sup>3/2</sup></small><span class="italic">ACD</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small><small><sub>Na</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small></td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">CV curves of the first cycle at various scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in NVPF/G (a), and NVPF/G@P (b). The first three turns of the NVPF/G@P are considered to be a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (c). Resistance comparison of NVPF, NVPF/G and NVPF/G@P (inset: equivalent circuit) (d).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>

        <hr class="hrule"><br>
      <p class="otherpara">In the formula, the peak current (mA) is denoted by <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>p</sub></small>, the effective contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte is denoted by A, the number of electrons per molecule during embedding is denoted by <span class="italic">n</span>, and the sodium ion concentration is denoted by <span class="italic">C</span>. The fitting results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. By comparison, the internal resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> = 4.71 Ω) and charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> = 147.82 Ω) of the NVPF/G@P samples were the smallest, indicating that the addition of PEK6K combined with the graphitic carbon layer could significantly reduce the internal resistance of NVPF/G@P as well as greatly enhance charge transfer.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical schematic of the whole cell consists of an NVPF/G@P positive electrode and HC negative electrode charge and discharge (see <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(a)</a>). The corresponding HC commercial anode hard carbon comes from KJ GROUP (Shenzhen Kejing Star Technology Company). The full cell charge and discharge diagram of NVPF/G@P//HC can be observed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(b)</a>; in this work, the specific N/P ratio in the full cells is 1.3 and the hard carbon loading in the full cell is about 1.5 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small>, and it can be clearly seen that the relatively stable charge and discharge platform and stable capacity greatly inherit the voltage platform of NVPF/G@P, and the discharge capacity of the 1st, 2nd, 5th and 10th times is 112.5, 112.4, 112.1 and 112.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(c) and (d)</a> show the rate capacity and corresponding charge–discharge curves of the NVPF/G@P//HC full-cells, respectively, and the capacities of the full-cells are 112.3, 100.6, 91.3, 89.3, 87.4, 79.3 and 76.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2C, 0.5C, 1, 2, 5, 10C and 20C, respectively. A relatively stable capacity after switching from 10C to 20C indicates that the full-cells exhibit excellent cycling stability, and after 100 cycles at 0.2C they still have a capacity of 91.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(e)</a>. In addition, the NVPF/G@P//HC full-cell can still have 85.0% coulombic efficiency after 100 cycles at 0.2C, indicating that it has good practical prospects.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nj05803d-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d3nj05803d/d3nj05803d-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) NVPF/G@P and HC electrodes in half cells. (b) Charge–discharge curves of NVPF/G@P//HC at 0.2C (1C = 143 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). (c) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at various current rates. (d) and (e) Rate capability and cycling performance of NVPF/G@P//HC full-cells.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect446"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>To sum up, a straightforward one-step ball milling synthesis route was used to prepare a uniform graphite carbon coated with high-stability NaVPO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F composites under the bonding of PEG6K. The as-obtained sample was a sodium-ion battery cathode material that delivers a high capacity of 128.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2C and a high rate capacity of 105.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10C, with a capacity retention rate of 116.0 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 200 cycles. Sodium-ion full-cells with NVPF/G@P and hard carbon as the positive and negative electrode materials exhibit excellent reversible sodium ion storage performance and very stable long-term cycling capacity. The outstanding performance of the NVPF/G@P cathode in both half-cells and full-cells positions the new composite material as a compelling choice for the next generation of high-performance advanced sodium-ion batteries.</span>
